     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Haggard's parents, Flossie Mae (Harp) and James Francis Haggard,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> moved to California from their home in <a href="/wiki/Checotah,_Oklahoma" title="Checotah, Oklahoma">Checotah, Oklahoma</a>, during the Great Depression, after their barn burned in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XVII_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XVII-8">[8]</a></sup> They settled with their children, Lowell and Lillian, in an apartment in <a href="/wiki/Bakersfield,_California" title="Bakersfield, California">Bakersfield</a>, while James started working for the <a href="/wiki/Atchison,_Topeka_and_Santa_Fe_Railway" title="Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway">Santa Fe Railroad</a>. A woman who owned a boxcar which was placed in <a href="/wiki/Oildale,_California" title="Oildale, California">Oildale</a>, a nearby town, asked Haggard's father about the possibility of converting it into a house. He remodeled the boxcar, and soon after moved in, also purchasing the lot, where Merle Ronald Haggard was born on April 6, 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel.2C_Michael_Karl.3B_Young-Witzel.2C_Gyvel2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DOq80iaT38k4C.26lpg.3DPA130.26dq.3Dlewell.2520guitar.2520merle.26pg.3DPA130.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_130.5D_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel.2C_Michael_Karl.3B_Young-Witzel.2C_Gyvel2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DOq80iaT38k4C.26lpg.3DPA130.26dq.3Dlewell.2520guitar.2520merle.26pg.3DPA130.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_130.5D-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XVIII_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XVIII-10">[10]</a></sup> The property was eventually expanded by building a bathroom, a second bedroom, a kitchen, and a breakfast nook in the adjacent lot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel.2C_Michael_Karl.3B_Young-Witzel.2C_Gyvel2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DOq80iaT38k4C.26lpg.3DPA130.26dq.3Dlewell.2520guitar.2520merle.26pg.3DPA130.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_130.5D_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel.2C_Michael_Karl.3B_Young-Witzel.2C_Gyvel2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DOq80iaT38k4C.26lpg.3DPA130.26dq.3Dlewell.2520guitar.2520merle.26pg.3DPA130.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_130.5D-9">[9]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>His father died of a brain hemorrhage in 1945,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XVIII_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XVIII-10">[10]</a></sup> an event that deeply affected Haggard during his childhood and the rest of his life. To support the family, his mother worked as a bookkeeper.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XX_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XX-11">[11]</a></sup> At 12, his brother, Lowell, gave him his used guitar. Haggard learned to play alone,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitzel.2C_Michael_Karl.3B_Young-Witzel.2C_Gyvel2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DOq80iaT38k4C.26lpg.3DPA130.26dq.3Dlewell.2520guitar.2520merle.26pg.3DPA130.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_130.5D_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitzel.2C_Michael_Karl.3B_Young-Witzel.2C_Gyvel2007.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DOq80iaT38k4C.26lpg.3DPA130.26dq.3Dlewell.2520guitar.2520merle.26pg.3DPA130.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_130.5D-9">[9]</a></sup> with the records he had at home, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Wills" title="Bob Wills">Bob Wills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lefty_Frizzell" title="Lefty Frizzell">Lefty Frizzell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hank_Williams" title="Hank Williams">Hank Williams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CMT_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMT-12">[12]</a></sup> As his mother was absent due to work, Haggard became progressively rebellious. His mother sent him for a weekend to a juvenile detention center to change his attitude, but it worsened.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XIX_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XIX-13">[13]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Haggard committed a number of minor offenses, such as thefts and writing bad checks. He was sent to a juvenile detention center for shoplifting in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> When he was 14, Haggard ran away to Texas with his friend Bob Teague.<sup id="cite_ref-CMT_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMT-12">[12]</a></sup> He <a href="/wiki/Freighthopping" title="Freighthopping">rode freight trains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hitchhike" title="Hitchhike" class="mw-redirect">hitchhiked</a> throughout the state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAronowitz.2C_Alfred_G.1968.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DCVUEAAAAMBAJ.26lpg.3DPA12.26dq.3Dmerle.2520haggard.2520bakersfield.2520singing.26pg.3DPA12.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_12.5D_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAronowitz.2C_Alfred_G.1968.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DCVUEAAAAMBAJ.26lpg.3DPA12.26dq.3Dmerle.2520haggard.2520bakersfield.2520singing.26pg.3DPA12.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_12.5D-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGleason.2C_Holly1988.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DDYcbla8ulxkC.26lpg.3DPA34.26dq.3Dmerle.2520haggard.2520potato.2520truck.26pg.3DPA34.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_34.5D_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGleason.2C_Holly1988.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DDYcbla8ulxkC.26lpg.3DPA34.26dq.3Dmerle.2520haggard.2520potato.2520truck.26pg.3DPA34.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_34.5D-16">[16]</a></sup> When he returned the same year, he and his friend were arrested for robbery. Haggard and Teague were released when the real robbers were found. Haggard was later sent to the juvenile detention center, from which he and his friend escaped again to <a href="/wiki/Modesto,_California" title="Modesto, California">Modesto, California</a>. He worked a series of laborer jobs, including driving a potato truck, being a short order cook, a hay pitcher, and an <a href="/wiki/Oil_well_shooting" title="Oil well shooting">oil well shooter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAronowitz.2C_Alfred_G.1968.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DCVUEAAAAMBAJ.26lpg.3DPA12.26dq.3Dmerle.2520haggard.2520bakersfield.2520singing.26pg.3DPA12.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_12.5D_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAronowitz.2C_Alfred_G.1968.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DCVUEAAAAMBAJ.26lpg.3DPA12.26dq.3Dmerle.2520haggard.2520bakersfield.2520singing.26pg.3DPA12.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_12.5D-15">[15]</a></sup> His debut performance was with Teague in a Modesto bar named "Fun Center", being paid US$5, with free beer.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He returned to Bakersfield in 1951, and was again arrested for truancy and <a href="/wiki/Petty_larceny" title="Petty larceny" class="mw-redirect">petty larceny</a> and sent to a juvenile detention center. After another escape, he was sent to the <a href="/wiki/Preston_School_of_Industry" title="Preston School of Industry">Preston School of Industry</a>, a high-security installation. He was released 15 months later, but was sent back after beating a local boy during a burglary attempt. After his release, Haggard and Teague saw Lefty Frizzell in concert. After hearing Haggard sing along to his songs backstage, Frizzell refused to sing unless Haggard would be allowed to sing first. He sang songs that were well received by the audience. Due to the positive reception, Haggard decided to pursue a career in music. While working as a farmhand or in oil fields, he played in nightclubs. He eventually landed a spot on the local television show <i>Chuck Wagon</i>, in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-CMT_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMT-12">[12]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Married and plagued by financial issues,<sup id="cite_ref-CMT_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CMT-12">[12]</a></sup> he was arrested in 1957 shortly after he tried to rob a Bakersfield roadhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKingsbury.2C_Paul2004223_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKingsbury.2C_Paul2004223-18">[18]</a></sup> He was sent to Bakersfield Jail,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XX_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XX-11">[11]</a></sup> and, after an escape attempt, was transferred to <a href="/wiki/San_Quentin_Prison" title="San Quentin Prison" class="mw-redirect">San Quentin Prison</a> on February 21, 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XXI_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XXI-19">[19]</a></sup> While in prison, Haggard discovered that his wife was expecting a child from another man, which pressed him psychologically. He was fired from a series of prison jobs, and planned to escape along with another inmate nicknamed "Rabbit". Haggard was convinced not to escape by fellow inmates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHochman.2C_Steve1999462_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHochman.2C_Steve1999462-20">[20]</a></sup> Haggard started to run a gambling and brewing racket with his cellmate. After he was caught drunk, he was sent for a week to solitary confinement where he encountered <a href="/wiki/Caryl_Chessman" title="Caryl Chessman">Caryl Chessman</a>, an author and death row inmate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEErlewine.2C_Stephen_Thomas.3B_Bogdanov.2C_Vladimir.3B_Woodstra.2C_Chris2003.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DLdxCPLZAExsC.26lpg.3DPA307.26dq.3Dgambling.2520and.2520brewing.2520merle.26pg.3DPA307.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_307.5D_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEErlewine.2C_Stephen_Thomas.3B_Bogdanov.2C_Vladimir.3B_Woodstra.2C_Chris2003.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DLdxCPLZAExsC.26lpg.3DPA307.26dq.3Dgambling.2520and.2520brewing.2520merle.26pg.3DPA307.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_307.5D-21">[21]</a></sup> Meanwhile, "Rabbit" had successfully escaped, only to shoot a police officer and return to San Quentin for execution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHochman.2C_Steve1999462_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHochman.2C_Steve1999462-20">[20]</a></sup> Chessman's predicament, along with the execution of "Rabbit", inspired Haggard to correct his life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEErlewine.2C_Stephen_Thomas.3B_Bogdanov.2C_Vladimir.3B_Woodstra.2C_Chris2003.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DLdxCPLZAExsC.26lpg.3DPA307.26dq.3Dgambling.2520and.2520brewing.2520merle.26pg.3DPA307.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_307.5D_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEErlewine.2C_Stephen_Thomas.3B_Bogdanov.2C_Vladimir.3B_Woodstra.2C_Chris2003.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DLdxCPLZAExsC.26lpg.3DPA307.26dq.3Dgambling.2520and.2520brewing.2520merle.26pg.3DPA307.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_307.5D-21">[21]</a></sup> Haggard soon earned a high school equivalency diploma and kept a steady job in the prison's textile plant,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEErlewine.2C_Stephen_Thomas.3B_Bogdanov.2C_Vladimir.3B_Woodstra.2C_Chris2003.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DLdxCPLZAExsC.26lpg.3DPA307.26dq.3Dgambling.2520and.2520brewing.2520merle.26pg.3DPA307.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_307.5D_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEErlewine.2C_Stephen_Thomas.3B_Bogdanov.2C_Vladimir.3B_Woodstra.2C_Chris2003.5Bhttps:.2F.2Fbooks.google.com.2Fbooks.3Fid.3DLdxCPLZAExsC.26lpg.3DPA307.26dq.3Dgambling.2520and.2520brewing.2520merle.26pg.3DPA307.23v.3Donepage.26q.26f.3Dfalse_307.5D-21">[21]</a></sup> while also playing for the prison's country music band,<sup id="cite_ref-bio_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bio-22">[22]</a></sup> attributing a 1958 performance by <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Cash" title="Johnny Cash">Johnny Cash</a> at the prison as his main inspiration to join it.<sup id="cite_ref-Haggard_Oldies_Bio_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haggard_Oldies_Bio-23">[23]</a></sup> He was released from San Quentin on parole in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1972, after Haggard had become an established country music star, then-California governor <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> granted Haggard a full and unconditional <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">pardon</a> for his past crimes.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg/170px-Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg" width="170" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg/255px-Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="318" data-file-height="325"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Merle_Haggard_Tally_Records_-_Cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Haggard depicted on a publicity portrait for Tally Records (1961, age 24).
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Upon his release, Haggard started digging ditches for his brother's electrical contracting company. Soon he was performing again, and later began recording with Tally Records. The Bakersfield Sound was developing in the area as a reaction against the over-produced <a href="/wiki/Honky_tonk" title="Honky tonk" class="mw-redirect">honky tonk</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Nashville_Sound" title="Nashville Sound" class="mw-redirect">Nashville Sound</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XXIII-XXVI_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XXIII-XXVI-26">[26]</a></sup> Haggard's first record for Tally was "Singing My Heart Out" b/w "Skid Row"; it was not a success, and only 200 copies were pressed. In 1962, Haggard wound up performing at a <a href="/wiki/Wynn_Stewart" title="Wynn Stewart">Wynn Stewart</a> show in <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Valley" title="Las Vegas Valley">Las Vegas</a> and heard Wynn's "Sing a Sad Song". He asked for permission to record it, and the resulting single was a national hit in 1964. The following year he had his first national top ten record with "<a href="/wiki/(My_Friends_Are_Gonna_Be)_Strangers" title="(My Friends Are Gonna Be) Strangers">(My Friends Are Gonna Be) Strangers</a>", written by <a href="/wiki/Liz_Anderson" title="Liz Anderson">Liz Anderson</a> (mother of country singer <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Anderson" title="Lynn Anderson">Lynn Anderson</a>) and his career was off and running.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XXVII-XXVIII_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECusic.2C_Don2002XXVII-XXVIII-27">[27]</a></sup> Haggard recalls having been talked into visiting Anderson—a woman he didn't know—at her house to hear her sing some songs she had written. "If there was anything I didn't wanna do, it was sit around some danged woman's house and listen to her cute little songs. But I went anyway. She was a pleasant enough lady, pretty, with a nice smile, but I was all set to be bored to death, even more so when she got out a whole bunch of songs and went over to an old pump organ...There they were. My God, one hit right after another. There must have been four or five number one songs there..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaggard.2C_Merle.2C_and_Peggy_Russell1981_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaggard.2C_Merle.2C_and_Peggy_Russell1981-28">[28]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1966, Haggard recorded "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_a_Lonesome_Fugitive" title="I'm a Lonesome Fugitive">I'm a Lonesome Fugitive</a>", also written by Liz Anderson with her husband Casey Anderson, which became his first #1 single.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> When the Andersons presented the song to Haggard, they were unaware of his prison stretch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013103_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013103-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_Owens" title="Bonnie Owens">Bonnie Owens</a>, Haggard's backup singer and then-wife, is quoted by music journalist Daniel Cooper in the liner notes to the 1994 retrospective <i>Down Every Road</i>: "I guess I didn't realize how much the experience at San Quentin did to him, 'cause he never talked about it all that much.... I could tell he was in a dark mood...and I said, 'Is everything okay?' And he said, 'I'm really scared.' And I said, 'Why?' And he said, 'Cause I'm afraid someday I'm gonna be out there...and there's gonna be...some prisoner...in there the same time I was in, stand up—and they're gonna be about the third row down—and say, 'What do you think you're doing, 45200?'" Cooper notes that the news had little effect on Haggard's career: "It's unclear when or where Merle first acknowledged to the public that his prison songs were rooted in personal history, for to his credit, he doesn't seem to have made some big splash announcement. In a May 1967 profile in <i>Music City News</i>, his prison record is never mentioned. But in July 1968, in the very same publication, it's spoken of as if it were common knowledge."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1962_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1962-31">[31]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The 1966 album <i>Branded Man</i> kicked off an artistically and commercially successful run for Haggard. In 2013 Haggard biographer David Cantwell stated, "The immediate successors to <i>I'm a Lonesome Fugitive</i> — <i>Branded Man</i> in 1967 and, in '68, <i><a href="/wiki/Sing_Me_Back_Home" title="Sing Me Back Home">Sing Me Back Home</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_Bonnie_and_Clyde" title="The Legend of Bonnie and Clyde" class="mw-redirect">The Legend of Bonnie and Clyde</a></i> — were among the finest albums of their respective years."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013125_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013125-32">[32]</a></sup> Haggard's new recordings largely centered around <a href="/wiki/Roy_Nichols" title="Roy Nichols">Roy Nichols</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Telecaster" title="Telecaster" class="mw-redirect">Telecaster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Mooney" title="Ralph Mooney">Ralph Mooney</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Steel_guitar" title="Steel guitar">steel guitar</a>, and the harmony vocals provided by Bonnie Owens. At the time of Haggard's first top-ten hit "<a href="/wiki/(My_Friends_Are_Gonna_Be)_Strangers" title="(My Friends Are Gonna Be) Strangers">(My Friends Are Gonna Be) Strangers</a>" in 1965, Owens (who had formerly been married to <a href="/wiki/Buck_Owens" title="Buck Owens">Buck Owens</a>) was known as a solo performer, a fixture on the <a href="/wiki/Bakersfield,_California" title="Bakersfield, California">Bakersfield</a> club scene who had appeared on television. She won the new <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Country_Music" title="Academy of Country Music">Academy of Country Music</a>'s first ever award for Female Vocalist after her 1965 debut album, <i>Don't Take Advantage of Me</i>, hit the top five on the country albums chart. However, Bonnie Owens had no further hit singles, and although she recorded six solo albums on Capitol between 1965 and 1970, she became mainly known for her background harmonies on Haggard hits like "<a href="/wiki/Sing_Me_Back_Home_(song)" title="Sing Me Back Home (song)">Sing Me Back Home</a>" and "Branded Man".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Producer Ken Nelson took a hands-off approach to producing Haggard. In the episode of <i><a href="/wiki/American_Masters" title="American Masters">American Masters</a></i> dedicated to him, Haggard remembers: "The producer I had at that time, Ken Nelson, was an exception to the rule. He called me 'Mr. Haggard' and I was a little twenty-four, twenty-five year old punk from Oildale...He gave me complete responsibility. I think if he'd jumped in and said, 'Oh, you can't do that,' it would've destroyed me."<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanMasters_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanMasters-34">[34]</a></sup> In the documentary series <i>Lost Highway</i>, Nelson recalls, "When I first started recording Merle, I became so enamored with his singing that I would forget what else was going on, and I suddenly realized, 'Wait a minute, there's musicians here you've got to worry about!' But his songs—he was a great writer."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Towards the end of the decade, Haggard composed several number 1 hits including "<a href="/wiki/Mama_Tried_(song)" title="Mama Tried (song)">Mama Tried</a>", "The Legend of Bonnie and Clyde", "Hungry Eyes", and "Sing Me Back Home".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013125_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013125-32">[32]</a></sup> Daniel Cooper calls "Sing Me Back Home", "a ballad that works on so many different levels of the soul it defies one's every attempt to analyze it."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1962_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1962-31">[31]</a></sup> In a 1977 interview in <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Bob_Eubanks" title="Bob Eubanks">Bob Eubanks</a>, Haggard reflected, "Even though the crime was brutal and the guy was an incorrigible criminal, it's a feeling you never forget when you see someone you know make that last walk. They bring him through the yard, and there's a guard in front and a guard behind—that's how you know a death prisoner. They brought Rabbit out...taking him to see the Father,...prior to his execution. That was a strong picture that was left in my mind." In 1968, Haggard's first tribute LP <i><a href="/wiki/Same_Train,_A_Different_Time" title="Same Train, A Different Time" class="mw-redirect">Same Train, Different Time: A Tribute to Jimmie Rodgers</a></i>, was also released to acclaim.</p> 
     <p>Haggard's songs attracted attention from outside the country field. <a href="/wiki/The_Everly_Brothers" title="The Everly Brothers">The Everly Brothers</a> covered both "Sing Me Back Home" and "Mama Tried" on their 1968 country-rock album <i><a href="/wiki/Roots_(Everly_Brothers_album)" title="Roots (Everly Brothers album)" class="mw-redirect">Roots</a></i>. The following year, Haggard's songs were performed and/or recorded by a variety of artists, including the <a href="/wiki/Gram_Parsons" title="Gram Parsons">Gram Parsons</a> incarnation of the <a href="/wiki/Byrds" title="Byrds" class="mw-redirect">Byrds</a>, who performed "<a href="/wiki/Sing_Me_Back_Home" title="Sing Me Back Home">Sing Me Back Home</a>" on the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ole_Opry" title="Grand Ole Opry">Grand Ole Opry</a> and recorded "Life in Prison" for their album <i><a href="/wiki/Sweetheart_of_the_Rodeo" title="Sweetheart of the Rodeo">Sweetheart of the Rodeo</a></i>; singer-activist <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">Joan Baez</a>, who covered "Sing Me Back Home" and "Mama Tried"; crooner <a href="/wiki/Dean_Martin" title="Dean Martin">Dean Martin</a>, who recorded "I Take a Lot of Pride in What I Am" for his album of the same name; and the <a href="/wiki/Grateful_Dead" title="Grateful Dead">Grateful Dead</a>, whose live cover of "Mama Tried" became a staple in their repertoire until the band's end in 1995. In the original <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> review for Haggard's 1968 album <i>Mama Tried</i>, Andy Wickham wrote, "His songs romanticize the hardships and tragedies of <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">America</a>'s transient proletarian and his success is resultant of his inherent ability to relate to his audience a commonplace experience with precisely the right emotional pitch...Merle Haggard looks the part and sounds the part because he is the part. He's great."</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Okie_from_Muskogee.22_and_.22The_Fightin.27_Side_of_Me.22">"Okie from Muskogee" and "The Fightin' Side of Me"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: &quot;Okie from Muskogee&quot; and &quot;The Fightin' Side of Me&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>In 1969, Haggard released "<a href="/wiki/Okie_from_Muskogee_(song)" title="Okie from Muskogee (song)">Okie From Muskogee</a>", with lyrics reflecting the singer's pride in being from <a href="/wiki/Middle_America_(United_States)" title="Middle America (United States)">Middle America</a> where people are considered patriotic, do not smoke <a href="/wiki/Marijuana" title="Marijuana" class="mw-redirect">marijuana</a>, take <a href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD" class="mw-redirect">LSD</a>, <a href="/wiki/Draft-card_burning" title="Draft-card burning">burn draft cards</a> or <a href="/wiki/Question_authority" title="Question authority">challenge authority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In the ensuing years, Haggard gave varying statements regarding whether he intended the song as a humorous satire or a serious political statement in support of conservative values.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In a 2001 interview, Haggard called the song a "documentation of the uneducated that lived in America at the time".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFox.2C_Aaron_A.200451_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFox.2C_Aaron_A.200451-38">[38]</a></sup> However, he made several other statements suggesting that he meant the song seriously. On the <i><a href="/wiki/Bob_Edwards_Show" title="Bob Edwards Show" class="mw-redirect">Bob Edwards Show</a></i>, he said, "I wrote it when I recently got out of the joint. I knew what it was like to lose my freedom, and I was getting really mad at these protesters. They didn't know anything more about the war in Vietnam than I did. I thought how my dad, who was from Oklahoma, would have felt. I felt I knew how those boys fighting in Vietnam felt."<sup id="cite_ref-kaufman_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kaufman-39">[39]</a></sup> In the country music documentary series <i>Lost Highway</i>, he elaborated: "My dad passed away when I was nine, and I don't know if you've ever thought about somebody you've lost and you say, 'I wonder what so-and-so would think about this?' I was drivin' on <a href="/wiki/Interstate_40" title="Interstate 40">Interstate 40</a> and I saw a sign that said "19 Miles to Muskogee". Muskogee was always referred to in my childhood as 'back home'. So I saw that sign and my whole childhood flashed before my eyes and I thought, 'I wonder what dad would think about the youthful uprising that was occurring at the time, the <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplins</a>...I understood 'em, I got along with it, but what if he was to come alive at this moment? And I thought, what a way to describe the kind of people in America that are still sittin' in the center of the country sayin', 'What is goin' on on these campuses?'"<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/American_Masters" title="American Masters">American Masters</a></i> documentary about him, he said, "That's how I got into it with the hippies...I thought they were unqualified to judge America, and I thought they were lookin' down their noses at something that I cherished very much, and it pissed me off. And I thought, 'You sons of bitches, you've never been restricted away from this great, wonderful country, and yet here you are in the streets bitchin' about things, protesting about a war that they didn't know any more about than I did. They weren't over there fightin' that war any more than I was."<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanMasters_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanMasters-34">[34]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Haggard began performing the song in concert in 1969 and was astounded at the reaction it received.</p> 
     <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td> 
        <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The Haggard camp knew they were on to something. Everywhere they went, every show, 'Okie' did more than prompt enthusiastic applause. There was an unanticipated adulation racing through the crowds now, standing ovations that went on and on and sometimes left the audience and the band-members alike teary-eyed. Merle had somehow stumbled upon a song that expressed previously inchoate fears, spoke out loud gripes and anxieties otherwise only whispered, and now people were using his song, were using <i>him</i>, to connect themselves to these larger concerns and to one another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013151_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013151-41">[41]</a></sup></td> 
        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td> 
       </tr> 
      </tbody>
     </table> 
     <p>The studio version, which was mellower than the usually raucous live concert versions, topped the country charts in 1969, where it remained for a month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013152_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013152-42">[42]</a></sup> It also hit number 41 on the <i>Billboard</i> all-genre singles chart, becoming Haggard's biggest hit up to that time (surpassed only by his 1973 crossover <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> hit, "<a href="/wiki/If_We_Make_It_Through_December" title="If We Make It Through December">If We Make It Through December</a>", which peaked at number 28)<sup id="cite_ref-haggardsongdb_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haggardsongdb-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hagbillboardchartp1_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hagbillboardchartp1-43">[43]</a></sup> and signature song.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>On his next single, "<a href="/wiki/The_Fightin%27_Side_of_Me" title="The Fightin' Side of Me">The Fightin' Side of Me</a>" (released in 1970 over Haggard's objections by his record company), Haggard's lyrics stated that he did not mind the counterculture "switchin' sides and standin' up for what they believe in" but resolutely declared, "If you don't love it, leave it!" In May 1970, Haggard explained to John Grissom of <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, "I don't like their views on life, their filth, their visible self-disrespect, y'know. They don't give a shit what they look like or what they smell like...What do they have to offer humanity?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013154_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013154-45">[45]</a></sup> In a 2003 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/No_Depression_(magazine)" title="No Depression (magazine)">No Depression</a></i> magazine, Haggard said, "I had different views in the '70s. As a human being, I've learned [more]. I have more culture now. I was dumb as a rock when I wrote 'Okie From Muskogee'. That's being honest with you at the moment, and a lot of things that I said [then] I sing with a different intention now. My views on marijuana have totally changed. I think we were brainwashed and I think anybody that doesn't know that needs to get up and read and look around, get their own information. It's a cooperative government project to make us think marijuana should be outlawed."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Ironically, Haggard had wanted to follow "Okie from Muskogee" with "Irma Jackson", a song that dealt with an interracial romance between a white man and an African-American woman. His producer, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Nelson_(United_States_record_producer)" title="Ken Nelson (United States record producer)">Ken Nelson</a>, discouraged him from releasing it as a single.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1962_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1962-31">[31]</a></sup> Jonathan Bernstein recounts, "Hoping to distance himself from the harshly right-wing image he had accrued in the wake of the hippie-bashing 'Muskogee', Haggard wanted to take a different direction and release "Irma Jackson" as his next single... When the Bakersfield, California native brought the song to his record label, executives were reportedly appalled. In the wake of 'Okie', Capitol Records was not interested in complicating Haggard's conservative, blue-collar image."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> After "The Fightin' Side of Me" was released instead, Haggard later commented to the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i>, "People are narrow-minded. Down South they might have called me a nigger lover."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013162_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013162-48">[48]</a></sup> In a 2001 interview, Haggard stated that Nelson, who was also head of the country division at Capitol at the time, never interfered with his music but "this one time he came out and said, 'Merle...I don't believe the world is ready for this yet'... And he might have been right. I might've canceled out where I was headed in my career."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1962_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1962-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>"Okie From Muskogee", "The Fightin' Side of Me", and "I Wonder If They Think of Me" (Haggard's 1973 song about an American <a href="/wiki/POW" title="POW" class="mw-redirect">POW</a> in Vietnam) were hailed as anthems of the <a href="/wiki/Silent_Majority" title="Silent Majority" class="mw-redirect">Silent Majority</a> and have been recognized as part of a recurring patriotic trend in American country music that also includes <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Daniels" title="Charlie Daniels">Charlie Daniels</a>' "In America" and <a href="/wiki/Lee_Greenwood" title="Lee Greenwood">Lee Greenwood</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/God_Bless_the_USA" title="God Bless the USA">God Bless the USA</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Friesen" title="Gordon Friesen">Gordon Friesen</a> of <i>Broadside</i> magazine criticized Haggard for his "[John] Birch-type songs against war dissenters", Haggard was popular with college students in the early 1970s, due not only to ironic use of his songs by <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture</a> members, but also because his music was recognized as coming from an early country-folk tradition. Both "Okie from Muskogee" and "The Fightin' Side of Me" received extensive airplay on underground radio stations, and "Okie" was performed in concert by <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest singers</a> <a href="/wiki/Arlo_Guthrie" title="Arlo Guthrie">Arlo Guthrie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phil_Ochs" title="Phil Ochs">Phil Ochs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kaufman_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kaufman-39">[39]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_career">Later career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Later career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg/170px-Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg" width="170" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg/255px-Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg/340px-Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="836"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Merle_Haggard_1975_-_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Haggard depicted on a publicity portrait for Capitol Records (1975, age 38).
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Haggard's LP <i><a href="/wiki/A_Tribute_to_the_Best_Damn_Fiddle_Player_in_the_World_(or,_My_Salute_to_Bob_Wills)" title="A Tribute to the Best Damn Fiddle Player in the World (or, My Salute to Bob Wills)">A Tribute to the Best Damn Fiddle Player in the World</a></i>, dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Bob_Wills" title="Bob Wills">Bob Wills</a>, helped spark a permanent revival and expanded audience for <a href="/wiki/Western_swing" title="Western swing">western swing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WillsCMT_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WillsCMT-53">[53]</a></sup> By this point, Haggard was one of the most famous country singers in the world, having enjoyed an immensely successful artistic and commercial run with Capitol, accumulating twenty-four number 1 country singles since 1966.</p> 
     <p>In 1972, <i>Let Me Tell You about A Song</i>, the first TV special starring Haggard, was nationally syndicated by Capital Cities TV Productions. It was a semi-autobiographical musical profile of Haggard, akin to the contemporary <i>Behind The Music</i>, produced and directed by Michael Davis. The 1973 <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a> anthem, "<a href="/wiki/If_We_Make_It_Through_December" title="If We Make It Through December">If We Make It Through December</a>", furthered Haggard's status as a champion of the working class. "If We Make It Through December" turned out to be Haggard's last crossover pop hit.<sup id="cite_ref-haggardsongdb_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haggardsongdb-1">[1]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Haggard appeared on the cover of <i>TIME</i> on May 6, 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> He also wrote and performed the theme song to the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Movin%27_On_(TV_series)" title="Movin' On (TV series)">Movin' On</a></i>, which in 1975 gave him another <a href="/wiki/Movin%27_On_(Merle_Haggard_song)" title="Movin' On (Merle Haggard song)">number one country hit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitburn.2C_Joel2006147_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitburn.2C_Joel2006147-55">[55]</a></sup> During the early to mid-1970s, Haggard's country chart domination continued with songs like "Someday We'll Look Back", "<a href="/wiki/Grandma_Harp" title="Grandma Harp">Grandma Harp</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Always_Wanting_You" title="Always Wanting You">Always Wanting You</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/The_Roots_of_My_Raising" title="The Roots of My Raising">The Roots of My Raising</a>". Between 1973 and 1976, he scored nine consecutive number 1 country hits. In 1977, he switched to <a href="/wiki/MCA_Records" title="MCA Records">MCA Records</a> and began exploring the themes of depression, alcoholism, and middle age on albums such as <i><a href="/wiki/Serving_190_Proof" title="Serving 190 Proof">Serving 190 Proof</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Way_I_Am_(Merle_Haggard_album)" title="The Way I Am (Merle Haggard album)">The Way I Am</a></i>. Haggard sang a duet cover of <a href="/wiki/Billy_Burnette" title="Billy Burnette">Billy Burnette</a>'s "What's A Little Love Between Friends" with <a href="/wiki/Lynda_Carter" title="Lynda Carter">Lynda Carter</a> in her 1980 television music special, <i>Lynda Carter: Encore!</i> He also scored a number 1 hit in 1980 with "<a href="/wiki/Bar_Room_Buddies" title="Bar Room Buddies">Bar Room Buddies</a>", a duet with actor <a href="/wiki/Clint_Eastwood" title="Clint Eastwood">Clint Eastwood</a> that appeared on the <i><a href="/wiki/Bronco_Billy" title="Bronco Billy">Bronco Billy</a></i> soundtrack.</p> 
     <p>Haggard appeared in an episode of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Waltons" title="The Waltons">The Waltons</a></i> entitled "The Comeback", season 5, episode 3, original air-date October 10, 1976. He played a band leader named Red, who had been depressed since the death of his son (Ron Howard).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>In 1981, Haggard published an autobiography, <i>Sing Me Back Home</i>. The same year, he alternately spoke and sang the ballad "The Man in the Mask". Written by <a href="/wiki/Dean_Pitchford" title="Dean Pitchford">Dean Pitchford</a> (whose other work includes "<a href="/wiki/Fame_(Irene_Cara_song)" title="Fame (Irene Cara song)">Fame</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Footloose_(song)" title="Footloose (song)">Footloose</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Sing_(1989_film)" title="Sing (1989 film)">Sing</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Solid_Gold_(TV_series)" title="Solid Gold (TV series)">Solid Gold</a>", and the musical <i><a href="/wiki/Carrie_(musical)" title="Carrie (musical)">Carrie</a></i>), this was the combined narration and theme for the movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Legend_of_the_Lone_Ranger" title="The Legend of the Lone Ranger">The Legend of the Lone Ranger</a></i>, a box-office flop. Haggard also changed record labels again in 1981, moving to Epic and releasing one of his most critically acclaimed albums, <i>Big City</i>.</p> 
     <p>Between 1981 and 1985, Haggard scored twelve more Top 10 country hits, with nine of them reaching number 1, including "My Favorite Memory," "Going Where the Lonely Go," "Someday When Things Are Good," and "Natural High." In addition, Haggard recorded two chart-topping duets with <a href="/wiki/George_Jones" title="George Jones">George Jones</a> ("Yesterdays' Wine" in 1982) and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Nelson" title="Willie Nelson">Willie Nelson</a> ("Pancho and Lefty" in 1983). Nelson believed the 1983 <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award" class="mw-redirect">Academy Award</a>-winning film <i><a href="/wiki/Tender_Mercies" title="Tender Mercies">Tender Mercies</a></i>, about the life of fictional singer Mac Sledge, was based on the life of Merle Haggard. Actor <a href="/wiki/Robert_Duvall" title="Robert Duvall">Robert Duvall</a> and other filmmakers denied this and claimed the character was based on nobody in particular. Duvall, however, said he was a big fan of Haggard.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1983, Haggard and his third wife Leona Williams divorced after five stormy years of marriage. The split served as a license to party for Haggard, who spent much of the next decade becoming mired in alcohol and drug problems.<sup id="cite_ref-heath_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heath-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013230_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantwell.2C_David2013230-58">[58]</a></sup> Haggard has stated that he was in the stages of his own <a href="/wiki/Mid-life_crisis" title="Mid-life crisis" class="mw-redirect">mid-life crisis</a>, or "male menopause," around this time. He said in an interview from this period: "Things that you've enjoyed for years don't seem nearly as important, and you're at war with yourself as to what's happening. 'Why don't I like that anymore? Why do I like this now?' And finally, I think you actually go through a biological change, you just, you become another...Your body is getting ready to die and your mind doesn't agree."<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanMasters_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanMasters-34">[34]</a></sup> He was briefly a heavy user of cocaine, but managed to kick the habit.<sup id="cite_ref-heath_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heath-57">[57]</a></sup> Despite these issues, he won a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Male_Country_Vocal_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Male Country Vocal Performance">Grammy Award for Best Male Country Vocal Performance</a> for his 1984 remake of "<a href="/wiki/That%27s_the_Way_Love_Goes_(Johnny_Rodriguez_song)" title="That's the Way Love Goes (Johnny Rodriguez song)">That's The Way Love Goes</a>."</p> 
     <p>Haggard was hampered by financial woes well into the 1990s, as his presence on the charts diminished in favor of newer country singers, such as <a href="/wiki/George_Strait" title="George Strait">George Strait</a> and <a href="/wiki/Randy_Travis" title="Randy Travis">Randy Travis</a>. Haggard's last number one hit was "<a href="/wiki/Twinkle,_Twinkle_Lucky_Star" title="Twinkle, Twinkle Lucky Star">Twinkle, Twinkle Lucky Star</a>" from his smash album <i>Chill Factor</i> in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1989, Haggard recorded a song, "Me and Crippled Soldiers Give a Damn", in response to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>'s decision to allow flag burning under the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a>. After CBS Records Nashville avoided releasing the song, Haggard bought his way out of the contract and signed with <a href="/wiki/Curb_Records" title="Curb Records">Curb Records</a>, which was willing to release the song. Haggard commented about the situation, "I've never been a guy that can do what people told me...It's always been my nature to fight the system."<sup id="cite_ref-Traditional_Values_NYT_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Traditional_Values_NYT-60">[60]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comeback">Comeback</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Comeback">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:MerleHaggardJun09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/MerleHaggardJun09.jpg/170px-MerleHaggardJun09.jpg" width="170" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/MerleHaggardJun09.jpg/255px-MerleHaggardJun09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/MerleHaggardJun09.jpg/340px-MerleHaggardJun09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2950"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:MerleHaggardJun09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Haggard performing in June 2009 (age 72).
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 2000, Haggard made a comeback of sorts, signing with the independent record label Anti and releasing the spare <i>If I Could Only Fly</i> to critical acclaim. He followed it in 2001 with <i>Roots, vol. 1</i>, a collection of <a href="/wiki/Lefty_Frizzell" title="Lefty Frizzell">Lefty Frizzell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hank_Williams" title="Hank Williams">Hank Williams</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hank_Thompson_(musician)" title="Hank Thompson (musician)">Hank Thompson</a> covers, along with three Haggard originals. The album, recorded in Haggard's living room with no overdubs, featured Haggard's longtime bandmates the Strangers as well as Frizzell's original lead guitarist, Norman Stephens. In December 2004, Haggard spoke at length on <i><a href="/wiki/Larry_King_Live" title="Larry King Live">Larry King Live</a></i> about his incarceration as a young man and said it was "hell" and "the scariest experience of my life".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>When political opponents were attacking the <a href="/wiki/Dixie_Chicks" title="Dixie Chicks">Dixie Chicks</a> for criticizing President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>'s invasion of Iraq, Haggard spoke up for the band on July 25, 2003, saying:</p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <p>I don't even know the Dixie Chicks, but I find it an insult for all the men and women who fought and died in past wars when almost the majority of America jumped down their throats for voicing an opinion. It was like a verbal witch-hunt and lynching.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>Haggard's number one hit single "Mama Tried" is featured in the 2003 film <i><a href="/wiki/Radio_(2003_film)" title="Radio (2003 film)">Radio</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Cuba_Gooding,_Jr." title="Cuba Gooding, Jr." class="mw-redirect">Cuba Gooding, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ed_Harris" title="Ed Harris">Ed Harris</a> as well as in Bryan Bertino's "The Strangers" with Liv Tyler. In addition, his song "Swingin' Doors" can be heard in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Crash_(2004_film)" title="Crash (2004 film)">Crash (2004)</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and his 1981 hit "Big City" is heard in Joel and Ethan Coen's film <i><a href="/wiki/Fargo_(film)" title="Fargo (film)">Fargo</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:20%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"> 
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;"> 
       <div> 
        <p>"He’s not going to play The Palace in Louisville, he’s going pick the tertiary towns, and he’s going to play on the outer edge. Merle was a real westerner. Like one of those lizards that thrives in arid heat. He was a California guy, but not the California you see on television with Palm Trees. He was the California that was dusty, that was Merle’s."<sup id="cite_ref-Owen13.04.16_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owen13.04.16-66">[66]</a></sup></p> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
      <cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Ketch Secor, <a href="/wiki/Old_Crow_Medicine_Show" title="Old Crow Medicine Show">Old Crow Medicine Show</a></cite>
     </div> 
     <p>In October 2005, Haggard released his album <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Wind_(Merle_Haggard_album)" title="Chicago Wind (Merle Haggard album)" class="mw-redirect">Chicago Wind</a></i> to mostly positive reviews. The album contained an anti-Iraq war song titled "America First," in which he laments the nation's economy and faltering infrastructure, applauds its soldiers, and sings, "Let's get out of Iraq, and get back on track." This follows from his 2003 release "Haggard Like Never Before" in which he includes a song, "That's The News". Haggard released a <a href="/wiki/Bluegrass_music" title="Bluegrass music">bluegrass</a> album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bluegrass_Sessions_(Merle_Haggard_album)" title="The Bluegrass Sessions (Merle Haggard album)">The Bluegrass Sessions</a></i>, on October 2, 2007.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>In 2008, Haggard was going to perform at Riverfest in <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock,_Arkansas" title="Little Rock, Arkansas">Little Rock, Arkansas</a>, but the concert was canceled because he was ailing, and three other concerts were canceled, as well; however, he was back on the road in June and successfully completed a tour that ended on October 19, 2008.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>In April 2010, Haggard released a new album, <i><a href="/wiki/I_Am_What_I_Am_(Merle_Haggard_album)" title="I Am What I Am (Merle Haggard album)">I Am What I Am</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> to strong reviews, and he performed the title song on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tonight_Show_with_Jay_Leno" title="The Tonight Show with Jay Leno">The Tonight Show with Jay Leno</a></i> in February 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaborations">Collaborations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Collaborations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Haggard collaborated with many other artists over the course of his career. In the early 1960s, Haggard recorded duets with Bonnie Owens (who later became his wife) for Tally Records, scoring a minor hit with "Just Between the Two of Us". As part of the deal that got Haggard signed to Capitol, producer <a href="/wiki/Ken_Nelson_(United_States_record_producer)" title="Ken Nelson (United States record producer)">Ken Nelson</a> obtained the rights to Haggard's Tally sides, including the duets with Owens, resulting in the release of Haggard's first duet album with Owens in 1966, also entitled <i>Just Between the Two of Us</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The album reached number 4 on the country charts, and Haggard and Owens recorded a number of additional duets before their divorce in 1978. Haggard went on to record duets with <a href="/wiki/George_Jones" title="George Jones">George Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Nelson" title="Willie Nelson">Willie Nelson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clint_Eastwood" title="Clint Eastwood">Clint Eastwood</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1970, Haggard released <i>A Tribute to the Best Damn Fiddle Player in the World (or, My Salute to Bob Wills)</i>, rounding up six of the remaining members of the Texas Playboys to record the tribute: Johnnie Lee Wills, Eldon Shamblin, Tiny Moore, Joe Holley, Johnny Gimble, and Alex Brashear.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks-52">[52]</a></sup> Merle's band the Strangers were also present during the recording but Wills suffered a massive stroke after the first day of recording. Merle arrived on the second day, devastated that he wouldn't get to record with him; the album helped return Wills to public consciousness, and set off a Western swing revival.<sup id="cite_ref-WillsCMT_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WillsCMT-53">[53]</a></sup> Haggard would do other tribute albums to Bob Wills over the next 40 years. In 1973 he appeared on <i>For the Last Time: Bob Wills and His Texas Playboys</i>. In 1994, Haggard collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Asleep_at_the_Wheel" title="Asleep at the Wheel">Asleep at the Wheel</a> and many other artists influenced by the music of Bob Wills on an album entitled <i>A Tribute To The Music of Bob Wills and the Texas Playboys</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> <i>A Tribute</i> was re-released on CD on the Koch label in 1995.</p> 
     <p>In 1972, Haggard agreed to produce <a href="/wiki/Gram_Parsons" title="Gram Parsons">Gram Parsons</a>' first solo album but backed out at the last minute. <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Brothers</a> arranged a meeting at Haggard's Bakersfield home and the two musicians seemed to hit it off but later, on the afternoon of the first session, Haggard canceled. Parsons, an enormous Haggard fan, was crushed, with his wife Gretchen telling Meyer, "Merle not producing Gram was probably one of the greatest disappointments in Gram's life. Merle was very nice, very sweet, but he had his own enemies and his own demons."<sup id="cite_ref-meyer_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1980, Haggard dispelled the notion that Parsons was any kind of outlaw, telling Mark Rose, "He was a pussy. Hell, he was just a long-haired kid. I thought he was a good writer. He was not wild, though. That's what's funny to me. All these guys running around in long hair talking about being wild and <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">Rolling Stones</a>. I don't think someone abusing themselves on drugs determines how wild they are. It might determine how ignorant they are."<sup id="cite_ref-meyer_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meyer-72">[72]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1982, Haggard recorded <i><a href="/wiki/A_Taste_of_Yesterday%27s_Wine" title="A Taste of Yesterday's Wine">A Taste of Yesterday's Wine</a></i> with George Jones, an album that produced two <a href="/wiki/Record_chart" title="Record chart">Top Ten</a> hits, including the number 1 "Yesterday's Wine."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In 2006, the pair released a sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/Kickin%27_Out_the_Footlights...Again" title="Kickin' Out the Footlights...Again">Kickin' Out the Footlights...Again</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Haggard released the duet album <i><a href="/wiki/Pancho_and_Lefty" title="Pancho and Lefty">Pancho and Lefty</a></i> with Willie Nelson in 1983, with the title track becoming an enormous hit for the duo. In 1987, a second, less successful LP, <i><a href="/wiki/Seashores_of_Old_Mexico" title="Seashores of Old Mexico">Seashores of Old Mexico</a></i>, was also released, and the pair would work together again with <a href="/wiki/Ray_Price_(musician)" title="Ray Price (musician)">Ray Price</a> in 2007, releasing the album <i>Last of a Breed</i>. In 2015 Haggard and Nelson recorded a video, "It's All Going to Pot," where both are seen singing in a recording studio while smoking joints and also recorded another duet album "Django and Jimmie".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1983, Haggard got permission from Epic Records to collaborate with then wife <a href="/wiki/Leona_Williams" title="Leona Williams">Leona Williams</a> on <a href="/wiki/Polydor_Records" title="Polydor Records">Polydor Records</a>, releasing <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_to_Heart_(Merle_Haggard_album)" title="Heart to Heart (Merle Haggard album)">Heart to Heart</a></i> in 1983. The album was not a hit, peaking at number 44.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2001, Haggard released an album of gospel songs with <a href="/wiki/Albert_E._Brumley" title="Albert E. Brumley">Albert E. Brumley</a> called <i>Two Old Friends</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In 2002, Haggard collaborated with longtime friend and fellow recording artist <a href="/wiki/Chester_Smith" title="Chester Smith">Chester Smith</a> (founder of television broadcasting company <a href="/wiki/Sainte_Partners_II,_L.P." title="Sainte Partners II, L.P.">Sainte Partners II, L.P.</a> and owner of several stations in California and Oregon) with a CD entitled <i>California Blend</i>. <sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The CD features classic country, western and gospel tracks performed by both Smith and Haggard.</p> 
     <p>In 2005, Haggard was featured as a guest vocalist on <a href="/wiki/Gretchen_Wilson" title="Gretchen Wilson">Gretchen Wilson</a>'s song "Politically Uncorrect".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> He is also featured singing a verse on <a href="/wiki/Eric_Church" title="Eric Church">Eric Church</a>'s 2006 song "Pledge Allegiance to the Hag".<sup id="cite_ref-yahr_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yahr-80">[80]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2005, Haggard was featured as a guest vocalist on <a href="/wiki/Blaine_Larsen" title="Blaine Larsen">Blaine Larsen</a>'s song "If Merle would sing my song".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Haggard endorsed <a href="/wiki/Fender_Musical_Instruments_Corporation" title="Fender Musical Instruments Corporation">Fender guitars</a> and had a Custom Artist signature model Telecaster. The guitar is a modified <a href="/wiki/Telecaster_Thinline" title="Telecaster Thinline" class="mw-redirect">Telecaster Thinline</a> with laminated top of figured maple, set neck with deep carved heel, birdseye maple fingerboard with 22 jumbo frets, ivoroid pickguard and binding, gold hardware, abalone Tuff Dog Tele peghead inlay, 2-Colour Sunburst finish, and a pair of Fender Texas Special Tele single-coil pickups with custom-wired 4-way pickup switching. He also played six-string acoustic models. In 2001, <a href="/wiki/C._F._Martin_%26_Company" title="C. F. Martin &amp; Company">C. F. Martin &amp; Company</a> introduced a limited edition Merle Haggard Signature Edition 000-28SMH acoustic guitar available with or without factory-installed electronics.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wives_and_children">Wives and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wives and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Haggard was married five times, first to Leona Hobbs from 1956 to 1964. They had four children: Dana, <a href="/wiki/Marty_Haggard" title="Marty Haggard">Marty</a>, Kelli, and <a href="/wiki/Noel_Haggard" title="Noel Haggard">Noel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Shortly after divorcing Hobbs, in 1965 he married singer <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_Owens" title="Bonnie Owens">Bonnie Owens</a>, former wife of Buck Owens, and a successful country singer at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Bonnie_Owens_Obituary_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonnie_Owens_Obituary-83">[83]</a></sup> Haggard credited her with helping him make his big break as a country artist. Haggard shared the writing credit with Owens for his hit "Today I Started Loving You Again", and acknowledged, including on stage, that the song was about a sudden burst of special feelings he experienced for her while they were touring together. She also helped care for Haggard's children from his first marriage, and was the maid of honor for Haggard's third marriage. Haggard and Owens divorced in 1978, but remained close friends as Owens continued as his backing vocalist until her death in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Bonnie_Owens_Obituary_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bonnie_Owens_Obituary-83">[83]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1978 Haggard married <a href="/wiki/Leona_Williams" title="Leona Williams">Leona Williams</a>; they divorced in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In 1985 Haggard married Debbie Parret; they divorced in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Fighter_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Fighter-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>He married his fifth wife, Theresa Ann Lane, on September 11, 1993. They had two children, Jenessa and Ben.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>A <a href="/wiki/Truck_driver" title="Truck driver">truck driver</a> and part-time musician named Scott Haggard has said that he is Merle Haggard's son from a brief relationship in 1968. According to Scott Haggard, a DNA test confirmed the paternity, but Scott and Merle Haggard had no contact until 2004, when they met and talked after a concert. After that, they had sporadic contact. As of Merle Haggard's death, his family had not confirmed or denied Scott Haggard's paternity.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cigarette_and_drug_use">Cigarette and drug use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cigarette and drug use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Haggard said he started smoking marijuana when he was 41 years old. He admitted that in 1983 he bought "$2,000 (worth) of cocaine" and partied for five months afterward, when he said he finally realized his condition and quit for good.<sup id="cite_ref-heath_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heath-57">[57]</a></sup> He quit smoking cigarettes in 1991, and stopped smoking marijuana in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> However, a <i>Rolling Stone</i> magazine interview in 2009 indicated that he had resumed regular marijuana smoking.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Fighter_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Fighter-85">[85]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness_and_death">Illness and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Illness and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Haggard underwent <a href="/wiki/Angioplasty" title="Angioplasty">angioplasty</a> in 1995 to unblock clogged arteries.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> On November 9, 2008, it was announced that he had been diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Non-small-cell_lung_cancer" title="Non-small-cell lung cancer" class="mw-redirect">non-small-cell lung cancer</a> in May and undergone surgery on November 3, during which part of his lung was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Haggard returned home on November 8.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Less than two months after his cancer surgery, he played two shows on January 2 and 3, 2009, in Bakersfield at <a href="/wiki/Buck_Owens_Crystal_Palace" title="Buck Owens Crystal Palace">Buck Owens Crystal Palace</a>, and continued to tour and record until his death.</p> 
     <p>On December 5, 2015, Haggard was treated at an undisclosed hospital in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> for <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pneumonia_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pneumonia-94">[94]</a></sup> He made a recovery but postponed several concerts.<sup id="cite_ref-pneumonia_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pneumonia-94">[94]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In March 2016, Haggard was once again hospitalized.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> His concerts for April were canceled due to his ongoing double pneumonia.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> On the morning of April 6, 2016, his 79th birthday, he died of complications from pneumonia at his home in <a href="/wiki/Palo_Cedro,_California" title="Palo Cedro, California">Palo Cedro, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nytobit_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytobit-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> His son Ben said that Haggard had predicted the day of his death a week prior.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Haggard was laid to rest in a private funeral at his ranch on April 9, 2016; longtime friend <a href="/wiki/Marty_Stuart" title="Marty Stuart">Marty Stuart</a> officiated.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_honors">Legacy and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legacy and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg/220px-Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg/330px-Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg/440px-Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="472" data-file-height="360"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Merle_Haggard_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Haggard at the White House for the 2010 Kennedy Center Honors
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During his long career, Haggard received numerous awards from the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Country_Music" title="Academy of Country Music">Academy of Country Music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Country_Music_Association" title="Country Music Association">Country Music Association</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Recording_Arts_and_Sciences" title="National Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences">National Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences</a> (<a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Awards</a>) (see <a href="#Awards">Awards</a>). He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Nashville_Songwriters_Hall_of_Fame" title="Nashville Songwriters Hall of Fame">Nashville Songwriters Hall of Fame</a> in 1977,<sup id="cite_ref-nashvillehof_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nashvillehof-3">[3]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Country_Music_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="Country Music Hall of Fame and Museum">Country Music Hall of Fame and Museum</a> in 1994,<sup id="cite_ref-countrymusichof_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrymusichof-4">[4]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_Music_Hall_of_Fame" title="Oklahoma Music Hall of Fame">Oklahoma Music Hall of Fame</a> in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-okmusichof_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-okmusichof-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2006, he received a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Lifetime_Achievement_Award" title="Grammy Lifetime Achievement Award">Grammy Lifetime Achievement Award</a>, and was also honored as a <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_Music_Incorporated" title="Broadcast Music Incorporated" class="mw-redirect">BMI</a> Icon at the 54th annual BMI Pop Awards that same year. During his songwriting career up to that time, Haggard had earned 48 BMI Country Awards, nine BMI Pop Awards, a BMI R&amp;B Award, and 16 BMI "Million-Air" awards, all from a catalog of songs that added up to over 25 million performances.<sup id="cite_ref-bmiawards_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmiawards-2">[2]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Haggard accepted a <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Center_Honors" title="Kennedy Center Honors">Kennedy Center Honor</a> on December 4, 2010, from the <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Center_for_the_Performing_Arts" title="John F. Kennedy Center for the Performing Arts">John F. Kennedy Center for the Performing Arts</a> in recognition of his lifetime achievement and "outstanding contribution to American culture".<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The following day he was honored at a gala in Washington, D.C. with musical performances by <a href="/wiki/Kris_Kristofferson" title="Kris Kristofferson">Kris Kristofferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Nelson" title="Willie Nelson">Willie Nelson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheryl_Crow" title="Sheryl Crow">Sheryl Crow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vince_Gill" title="Vince Gill">Vince Gill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamey_Johnson" title="Jamey Johnson">Jamey Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kid_Rock" title="Kid Rock">Kid Rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miranda_Lambert" title="Miranda Lambert">Miranda Lambert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brad_Paisley" title="Brad Paisley">Brad Paisley</a>; this tribute was featured on the December 28, 2010, CBS telecast of the Kennedy Center Honors.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In July 2007, a 3 1/2 mile stretch of 7th Standard Road in Oildale, California, where Haggard grew up, was renamed "Merle Haggard Drive" in his honor. Merle Haggard Drive stretches from North Chester Avenue west to <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_99" title="U.S. Route 99">U.S. Route 99</a> and provides access to the William M. Thomas airport terminal at <a href="/wiki/Meadows_Field_Airport" title="Meadows Field Airport">Meadows Field Airport</a>. Haggard played two shows to raise money to pay for the changes in road signage.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In 2015, the converted boxcar in which the Haggard family lived in Oildale was moved to the <a href="/wiki/Kern_County_Museum" title="Kern County Museum">Kern County Museum</a> for historic preservation and restoration.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>On June 14, 2013, the <a href="/wiki/California_State_University,_Bakersfield" title="California State University, Bakersfield">California State University, Bakersfield</a>, awarded Haggard the <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary degree</a> of Doctor of Fine Arts. Haggard stepped to the podium and said, "Thank you. It's nice to be noticed."</p> 
     <p>On January 26, 2014, Haggard performed his 1969 song "<a href="/wiki/Okie_from_Muskogee" title="Okie from Muskogee">Okie from Muskogee</a>" at the <a href="/wiki/56th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="56th Annual Grammy Awards">56th Annual Grammy Awards</a> along with Kris Kristofferson, Willie Nelson, and <a href="/wiki/Blake_Shelton" title="Blake Shelton">Blake Shelton</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Merle_Haggard&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Haggard's guitar playing and voice gave his country songs a hard-edged, blues-like style in many cuts. Although he was outspoken in his dislike for modern country music,<sup id="cite_ref-yahr_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yahr-80">[80]</a></sup> he praised <a href="/wiki/George_Strait" title="George Strait">George Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toby_Keith" title="Toby Keith">Toby Keith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Jackson" title="Alan Jackson">Alan Jackson</a>. Keith has singled out Haggard as a major influence on his career.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">

        <a href="/wiki/Oildale,_California" title="Oildale, California">Oildale, California</a>
